 KUNGL. VITTERHETS HISTORIE OCH ANTIKVITETS AKADEMIEN STOCKHOLM REVELATIONES SANCTAE BIRGITTAE REVELACIONES LIB. VIII LIBER CELESTIS IMPERATORIS AD REGES EDIDIT HANS AILI 
 
 
 
 
 SANCTA BIRGITTA REVELACIONES BOOK VIII LIBER CELESTIS IMPERATORIS AD REGES EDITED BY HANS AILI 
 
 
 
 
 Published with grants from the Swedish Research Council AILI, H., Sancta Birgitta, Reuelaciones, Book VIII, Liber celestis im- peratoris ad reges. Svenska fornskriftsällskapet and Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och Antikvitets Akademien. Stockholm 2002. 229 pages. ISBN 91-973803-3-4 This is the first critical edition of Book VIII of the Reuelaciones of St. Birgitta of Sweden, a book that was originally conceived by its medieval editor, Alfonso of Jaen, as a Mirror of Kings, selected from 58 of Bir- gitta's Revelations and entitled Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges. The edition bases its text upon the readings of selected medieval manu- scripts. The text of the Editio Princeps (impr. B. Ghotan, Liibeck 1492), which is based on two manuscripts belonging to Vadstena Abbey, is here superseded by a text much closer to Alfonso's original version. This edition also contains a reprint of Alfonso's Preface entitled Epistola Solitarii ad reges (ed. Arne Jönsson 1989), an Apparatus criti- cus, an Introduction with Bibliography, an Apparatus fontium, a Glos- sary, and indices. Keywords: St. Birgitta, St. Bridget of Sweden, Revelation, Reuela- ciones, Vadstena, Spirituality. Hans Aili, Department of Classical Languages, Stockholm University, SE-106 91 Stockholm, Sweden. Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och Antikvitets Akademien, Box 5622, SE-114 86 Stockholm, Sweden. ISSN 0347-9145 ISBN 91-973803-3-4 Printed in Sweden by AB C 0 Ekblad & Co, Västervik 2002

 Preface This edition of St. Birgitta's Mirror of Kings, as compiled and edited by her Spanish father-confessor, Alfonso Pecha, has been long in the making. At its outset, in the early nineteen-nineties, the project was financed for three years by generous grants from Statens Humanistisk-Samhällsvetenskapliga forskningsråd (HSFR), while my travels to the libraries that house the manuscripts were supported by the Royal Academy of Letters, History and Antiquities (Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och Antikvitets Akademien) of Stock- holm. From then on, I have worked on the edition whenever my teaching and adminis- trative duties at Stockholm University have permitted. At the intellectual level my thanks are due, first and foremost, to the members of the Birgitta Committee of the Academy, professors Birger Bergh (Lund) and Jan Öberg (Stockholm), and to the members of the Latin seminars of the Universities of Gothen- burg, Lund, Stockholm, and Uppsala, where I have from time to time presented parts of my edition. The hospitality and help given me by the staffs of the respective manuscript or research libraries will always remain a pleasant memory to me. Mr David Jones kind- ly undertook the much-needed task of correcting the English of my Introduction. Re- maining errors and errors committed after his revision are, needless to say, my own re- sponsibility. My wife, Christina, and my sons, Daniel and Eric, provided the happy and carefree background so necessary for scholarly work. Stockholmiae, mense Januario anno Domini MMII. Hans Aili

 1. Introduction 1.1 The Liber celestis Imperatoris ad reges and its editor, Alfonso Pecha Liber celestis Imperatoris ad reges is, in the later manuscripts as weil as in the printed editions from Gh and onward, usually described as Book VIII of the Corpus Reuela- cionum Sancte Birgitte.1 It is a compilation, the work of Birgitta's Spanish father confes- sor, the hermit Alfonso Pecha (1327 to 1389),2 formerly bishop of Ja&I, containing 58 revelations, selected partly from those already belonging to the earlier books, partly from such as had not been included in the existing corpus. The work of selection was proba- bly completed only a very short time after Alfonso had finished his work on the original Liber celestis Reuelacionum which included Books I to VII. Alfonso added his own pref- ace, entitled Epistola Solitarii ad reges (abbreviated ES), to his compilation. Alfonso's life, works, and politics have been the subject of a number of modern papers and monographs, notably SEIDLMEYER (1930), COLLEDGE (1956), JÖNSSON A. (1989), and GILICER (1993).3 The two latter works in particular afford important insights into the life and character of this remarkable man. A more modest discussion of his editorial tech- niques is given in a conference paper by Mu (2000). Epistola Solitarii ad reges, Alfon- so's Preface to the Liber ad reges, is discussed by VOADEN (1999). JÖNSSON A. (1997) demonstrates that Alfonso's editorship was not limited to the Mir- ror of Kings, as he also compiled the Tractatus de summis pontificibus. This collection of short Revelations (13 in number) never reached the mainstream corpus as a separate work, nor does it appear in the Italian manuscripts of the Revelations; it was eventually printed in the Editio princeps by B. Ghotan, of 1492, as chapters 132 to 144 of Book IV. This edition of the Liber ad reges, henceforth abbreviated LAR, adheres on the whole to the principfes laid down in the other Birgittine editions that have appeared to date. Most of the problems concerning the selection of manuscripts and the history of the text having been resolved by previous editors and Birgittine scholars, we shall, in this intro- ductory chapter, restrict discussioin to new questions that relate particularly to the text of LAR. The present state of the editorial work on the Revelations will appear from the Bibliography, s.v. Birgitta. The edition of Book II was started by C.G. UNDHAGEN; after his untimely death, the work was taken over by B. BERGH, and the edition was published in 2001. A modern, critical edition of the Epistola Solitarii and other Al- fonsine texts was published by JÖNSSON, A. (1989). GILICER (1993:41), quoting one Spanish authority puts his birth at 1327, while JÖNSSON, A. (1989:32), citing another Spanish source gives it as 1329/30. 3 Including an animated discussion between the two latter scholars: see JÖNSSON A. (1995) and GILKIER (1996). 
 
 
 
 
 18 Since it is desirable that this text, originally compiled and edited by Alfonso, should also include his Prologue, the latter work is reproduced here from JÖNSSON'S edition and with an abbreviated apparatus criticus.4 1.2 The History of the Text The discussion among Birgittine scholars concerning the dating of the Revelations actu- ally follows two separate tracks, namely the dates when the separate books were com- piled by Alfonso and the time when they were entered into the Corpus itself. Chronological aspects of this process ought to be set out clearly before we discuss its implications. Pope Gregory XI entered Rome on January i 1377;5 within a very short time he was presented with a copy of the Revelations, an action which resulted in the first proposal for the canonization of Birgitta, put forward on May 29th of the same year by John of Spain, magister in sacra pagina.6 This means that a complete manuscript of the Revelations was in existence very close to the date of the Pope's return; this can only have been copied during the preceding year and may even have existed as a finished text in 1375. UNDHAGEN (1978:16) convincingly contended that the corpus of Revelations that had been first submitted to Gregory XI was not changed or extended before it was presented to the new pope, Urban VI, in 1379, and that this corpus contained the texts of the First Redaction. It is difficult to find fault with his reasoning. At the same time, we may re- mark that this in no way precludes the theory that Alfonso had at that time actually fin- ished editing LAR and had also written ES but had not, as yet, incorporated these texts fully into the Corpus Reuelacionum itself. A. JÖNSSON (1989:85ff.) proposes that the Liber ad reges was finished as early as ca. 1375 and the Epistola Solitarii in October 1376 (at the latest). In doing so he differs rad- ically from the earlier Birgittine scholars, SCHUCK, WESTMAN, and UNDHAGEN, who date this prologue to 1377-1379, before 1377, and after 1377 respectively.7 On the other hand, he makes no explicit effort to follow up his theory of the dating of these two works by proposing a date for when they were entered into the Birgittine corpus. The long discussion on the second point was summarized by UNDHAGEN (1978:1ff.), who concluded by dating the first collection of Revelations, which he dubbed the First Alfonsine Redaction (containing Books I to VII) to 1377, and the Second Redaction (containing Books I to VII, Epistola Solitarii ad reges, Liber ad reges, Sermo Angelicus, and Quattuor oraciones) to ca 1380.8 UNDHAGEN'S reconstruction of the contents of the First Redaction is, on the whole, The observant reader will note that the orthography of the Epistola Solitarii as edited by JÖNSSON differs in minor ways from that of the main body the Liber ad reges as edited by myself, the former showing a normal- ized medieval spelling (JÖNSSON 1989:113), whereas mine follows in the main the spelling of the Codex Kalmarnensis, a principle I adopted in my edition of Book IV (see Am 1992:53). CAPPELLI (1983:268). 6 A&P p. 4. 7 See A. JöNssoN's discussion (1989:87). UNDHAGEN (1978:24). 
 
 
 
 
 19 convincing, particularly his reference to Alfonso's own words in ES 8:5 in illo magno vo- lumine Libri Celestis, in quo septem libri continentur, multe reuelaciones ad reges et im- peratores pertinentes incluse iacebant. Although these words do not put a date to the con- ception of ES and LAR, they nonetheless clearly show that Alfonso at a certain point in time saw the Liber ad reges as a work that, while still separate, derived from the Liber celestis. In a separate work,9 I have argued that Alfonso's positioning of the LAR as book eight, thus demoting Book VII from its traditional position as Liber ultimus, provoked the cre- ator of the so-called Prague redaction — whom UNDHAGEN (1978:32) identifies with Matheus de Cracovia — into inserting the LAR between books II and III in his own redac- tion, in the same way as he inserted another "supernumerary" work, the Sermo Angeli- cus, between Books IV and V. In his case we are dealing with a delayed chronology, as he probably received his first Revelations manuscript (containg the First Redaction) about 1379, and cannot reasonably be assumed to have found out about Alfonso's addi- tions until 1380 or 1381.1° Thus, these observations merely indicate that Alfonso's First Redaction was made a certain period of time before the genesis of LAR (and ES) and the other works associated with the Second Redaction. Fixing this temporal point is more problematical. JÖNSSON, opposing UNDHAGEN'S dat- ing of the Second Redaction, which tends to put the assumption of ES and LAR into the Corpus to after 1379 (a consequence of his theory that both Popes received the same col- lection of works), draws attention to two facts (1989:86 and 88f.). Firstly, UNDHAGEN (1978:26) argued that Book VII, in the majority of the manuscripts, is dubbed ultimus liber celestis. Against this JÖNSSON notes "It is certainly true that the seventh book is called the last book in A&P, also by Alfonso himself, but all the same, he illogically calls the Liber Celestis Imperatoris ad Reges the eighth book. At the same time it was thus possible to call the seventh book the last book and to call the Liber Celestis Imperatoris ad Reges the eighth book, a fact that considerably reduces the worth of Schtick's and Undhagen's arguments." JÖNSSON here refers to A&P, p. 375,1. 32, the text of which runs: Sed quia multi non jmerito dubitabant, quomodo et qualiter predicta domina Brigida poterat habere et habebat frequentissime in oracionibus suis tantam habundanciam reuelacionum et diuinarum visionum, que continentur in libris su- is, jdeo ipse testis dixit ad certificandum seipsum et alios animales homines et car- nales, qui spiritualia sublimia non sapiunt nec percipiunt, factus et compositus fult prologus hoc plene declarans per diuinam scripturam super primo libro ce- lesti et clarius super viij°11 eidem domine Brigide reuelato, qui intitulatur liber ce- lestis jmperatoris ad reges, et ibi plene declaratur de hac materia ... Alfonso submitted this testimony on September 16th, 1379,12 that is, in the autumn of the year that the case for Birgitta's canonization was presented to Pope Urban VI. The above remark tells us that at this date Alfonso himself regarded LAR and its Prologue as part of 9 AILI-SVANBERG, Imagines Sanctae Birgittae, forthcoming. 1° AILI-SVANBERG, Imagines Sanctae Birgittae, forthcoming; UNDHAGEN (1978:31ff.) According to the apparatur criticus viip is a correction, by a later hand, from vjt°. 12 A&P p. 363. 
 
 
 
 
 20 the series of Revelations. It is furthermore noteworthy that Alfonso clearly calls Book I Primus liber celestis but Book VIII merely "the eighth book revealed to the Lady Birgit- ta": in this perspective, Book VII could still be called Ultimus liber celestis. As we have seen above, even in ES Alfonso explicitly states that the Liber celestis contains seven vol- umes. This does not pose a real problem: it only indicates that Alfonso himself distin- guished between the Liber celestis proper, which contained seven books, and the other Birgittine texts, among which the Liber celestis ad reges held a prominent place. His ter- minology in the matter of the different works of the Corpus Birgittinum is therefore per- fectly consistent. We may also note that Alfonso's reference to the Liber ad reges in his deposition in- evitably means that in 1379 he had submitted this text and its Prologue to the second ex- amining commission — otherwise there would have been little reason to mention it at all in his deposition — but that these works were probably still seen by him as two separate entities, the Liber celestis and the Liber celestis ad reges. Returning to the choronology offered by JÖNSSON concerning the point of time when ES and LAR were actually finished, we may comment that he offers a second objection to UNDHAGEN's theory. It is based on an observation that also puzzled the latter scholar. A letter written at Vadstena in 142113 and intended as an answer to a letter containing questions sent from a frater of Syon Abbey appears to state that eight books of revela- tions were handed over to Gregory XI. This Pope died on March 27, 1378. The informa- tion given in this letter must be treated with caution, however: the key phrase illud mag- num volumen reuelacionum continens viij fibros is a quote carried over from the original letter from England. There is therefore first of all a double likelihood of viij being a sim- ple error of transcription. Secondly, as UNDHAGEN remarks in his note, the questioner at Syon Abbey apparently believed that Birgitta had written one, very large volume divided into 14 books ; as there is one extant manuscript of English origins with precisely this di- vision, namely L,14 UNDHAGEN correctly notes that the letter helps to date this English redaction. Thirdly, it is a curious fact, not commented on by either UNDHAGEN nor JÖNS- SON, that the probable writer of the letter in question is the same Ericus lohannis, Con- fessor General to Vadstena Abbey, who in 1417 exhibited the Liber Alfonsi (with its sev- en books of Revelations) to a number of Swedish clerics. Why, then, did he not react against the numeral viij? Did he consider it a trifling scribal error? Surely he would have been the best person to correct his English correspondent? Or was he convinced that the second commission actually received eight books (as they were regarded in his own time) for consideration? Against these questions we may note, however, as JÖNSSON points out, that the Birgittines themselves sometimes made remarkable errors concerning their own history.' In actual fact all the external criteria that JöNssoN16 lists — with one important excep- tion — serve to set the terminus ante quem for the conclusion of the Epistola Solitarii to the death of Gregory XI, that is March 27th, 1378. This, as we have seen, is fully con- vincing, albeit not very helpful. (1978:21, note 81), who attributes it to the Confessor General of that time, Ericus lohannis. London, British Library, MS Harley 612. 15 A. JÖNSSON (1989:88). 16 A. JÖNSSON (1989:105ff). 13 UNDHAGEN 14 
 
 
 
 
 21 Only the last argument in JÖNSSON'S list is intended to point to an even earlier dating. He there adduces some phrases used in the three petitions for canonization made by the King of Sweden and his foremost officials on October 9th, 1376,17 by Queen Giovanna of Naples in October, 1376,18 and by the Emperor Charles IV on September 911 , 1377.19 He argues that the wording of these three petitions may in minor ways have been influenced by the wording of ES, but does not discuss the possibility of the opposite process, which is that Alfonso picked phrases from the petitions and used them in his Prologue. This is all the more remarkable when we notice that Alfonso himself in the Epistola Solitarii2° mentions that examinations had already been performed in Sweden and at Naples. UND- HAGEN21 uses Alfonso's reference to these two examinations, and his failure to mention the commission of Gregory XI, as grounds for suspecting that ES and LAR were drafted even before this commission was appointed in 1377. We might add that Alfonso's failure to mention the Emperor's petition of September 1377 adds even more strength to this ar- gument. An artistic argument, adduced by NORDENFALK (1961:379 with note 21), appears to open up the possibility that both P and Y can be dated earlier than we have done. He refers to a typewritten description of Y, produced at the Pierpont Morgan Library, stating that the "costumes and the helmets used serve to date the manuscript as early as ca. 1375". NORDENFALK concedes this possibility without expanding on it. Undoubtedly, this serves as a terminus post quem for the genesis of the illuminations and miniatures of these manuscripts; it probably even indicates when the decorations were first conceived; but this argument alone cannot date the manuscripts, as we must allow for a certain con- servatism in the manner of execution, once the artistic pattern had been set by the mas- ter illuminator. In conclusion, the weight of the evidence makes it reasonable to date the Liber Celestis Imperatoris ad Reges, with its Prologue, to between the autumn of 1376 and the autumn of 1377. When and how it was adopted into the main corpus of Birgittine texts is less cer- tain: the evidence points towards its having been included in the material submitted to the commission appointed by Pope Urban VI in 1379, although it was probably still regard- ed as a separate entity and not a part of the Liber celestis. 1.3 The Geminated Revelations Of the fifty-eight chapters constituting the text of Book, no less than twenty-five have been culled, by Alfonso, in their entirety from books II to VII, while a further three have been used in extenso. Having been adopted, the Revelations were normally adapted, also by Alfonso, for their new purpose within a Mirror of Kings. The adaptation took the form of a thematic replacement of words or phrases: an overview of Alfonso's procedures and (1998 No. 85, p. 117f. and 159), A&P p. 48f., SRS, vol. I:1 p. 224. A&P p. 54f. COLLIJN'S dating of 1377 was corrected by EKWALL (1965, p. 100 n. 7). 19 A&P p. 53f. 20 ES VI: 8-11. 21 UNDHAGEN (1978:24, note 96). 17 18 STAHL 
 
 
 
 
 22 his successes and failures as editor of this book has previously been presented in out- line,22 and is given with more detail in a separate paper.23 The presence of a large number of geminated chapters eventually created a resistance among the scribes, who appear to have reacted against the compulsion to write these texts twice over. Among the manuscripts of Italian origins,24 P, Y, N give the full text in both places, and the manuscripts of the [32 tradition follow the same pattern, while r25 omits the relevant chapters of Book VII, where the reader is referred to Book VIII. In the manuscripts outside this tradition, however, a process of simplification soon set in. The manuscripts of Vadstena provenance handle the problem in an individual fashion: F rather erratically sometimes refers the reader to Book VIII, sometimes gives the text in both places, sometimes even refers the reader from Book VIII to the earlier location. Pos- sibly because of this lack of principle, Rev. IV 74 (= VIII 34) is missing in both books. K consistently gives the text of Book VIII but has no clear principle in the retention or omission of the text in the earlier books — the matter is complicated by the fact that a sec- ond hand, dubbed K2, at the end of the codex gives the text of all those chapters that were left out in the earlier books (again excessively providing the text of Rev. II 12 and 13, al- though these are found in their proper places). The manuscripts of the Prague Redaction also give the text fully in Book VIII but omit or retain the text erratically in the earlier books, a practice that is also followed, in its particular fashion, by q. In the Ghotan edi- tion, finally, the text is consistently given at the first place of occurrence, no geminated chapters being printed in their complete shape in Book VIII.26 However, owing to the in- fluence of K2, the text given does not consistently belong either that of the earlier books or to that of the Liber ad reges, but very often to that only given by K2 — for a discussion of this phenomenon, see AiLi (2000). Chapters 33, 35, and 36 form a particular problem, as their texts were culled from Book II 8, 13, and 12 (in that order) respectively; however, Alfonso did not use the en- tire text but removed large, undesired sections: details of his procedure are given in the apparatus criticus of each chapter. This action on Alfonso's part is not as clearly reflect- ed by the manuscripts or the Editio princeps as is the case in the truly geminated chap- ters. In my edition, I give the relevant paragraph numbers of II 8, 12, and 13 within square brackets, in order to facilitate reference. ALLT (1992:44-47) and Am (1986). Structurally, this paper manuscript is arranged in two parts, the first of which gives the text of Rev. I to III en suite, while Rev. IV to VII all end on the final verso page of a gath- ering, the text of the following book beginning on the first page of the new gathering. It is also noteworthy that the composer managed to fit the text of Rev. V into one single sex- tern, while he used seven sheets to reach the same result in Rev. VII. This seems to indi- cate that the principles, governing the relation between structure and text, that can be ob- served in P and Y3° were not abandoned completely in the paper manuscripts. Undhagen dated N to the first half of the 1400s. This codex is remarkably unconsis- tent in its Birgittine nomenclature, Birgitta herself being called either Beata or Sancta Brigitta. In manuscripts produced before her canonization, like P and Y used for the present edition, she is regularly called Sancta Brigida, while the scribes of the younger manuscripts are normally careful to to take note of her elevation. In N, she is called Sanc- ta Brigitta on fol. 1R, 2R, and 64R, but Beata Brigitta on fol. 13R, 78V, 122V (twice), 170V, and 185R; on fol. 16R she is dubbed beate memorie domina Brigitta de Suecia, on (19788:118) having noted the absence of a series of rubrics belonging to Rev.  2. The Manuscripts and their Mutual Relationships 2.1 Introductory Remarks The manuscripts used as a basis for this edition are nine, in alphabetical order, B F K N P Y q r u, and the editio princeps, Gh. A comparison with the list of manuscripts col- lated by A.-M. Jönsson for Book III,31 those collated by myself for Book IV32 and those collated by BERGH for Book VI,33 will show that in the present edition u alone represents the Prague redaction (otherwise represented by P, x and v), while B in the same fashion alone represents the 132 group. N, finally, is a newcomer as a textual witness. In the edi- tion of Book IV34 u was described in print for the first time and was demonstrated to be of greater value as a textual witness, as compared to that of the other Prague manuscripts, Ib, x, and v. The E32 group, represented by B, 0 and Ab35 has a limited value for the es- tablishment of the text, as it contains little that is not also present in the other manuscripts, but offers instead a great number of scribal corrections of doubtful value. Among these three, B appears to be the most rewarding object for collation, as Ab offers a wealth of complications without the hope of any commensurate reward,36 while 0 does not contain the Liber ad reges at all and thus eliminates itself from consideration. The readings of Ab are, thus, not used in the present edition, although they are given in the following discussion on the manuscript relations. With these considerations in mind we found it more useful and potentially more inter- esting to collate N in the hope that it might help shed further light on the history of the Italian textual tradition. The history of this manuscript itself added to its interest, for, as UNDHAGEN37 has pointed out, it, alone among those of the same textual tradition, is of manifest Italian origins; during the Middle Ages, it belonged to the library of the Palaz- B F K OPVY Ab Tb q u v x for the main body of text, with the addition of Ua for Rev. III 12, 19, 22. B F K OPVY Ab q u x for the main body of text, with the addition of Ua for Rev. IV 131-144 (where the b group fails: cf. also A. JÖNSSON 1997). F K O P Y Ab Fb Pqr sv for the main body of text, with the addition of Ua for Rev VI 110-122 (where the b group fails). 34 Am (1992:28ff. and 38). 35 0 = Oxford, Balliol College Library, MS 225, Ab = Darmstadt, Hessische Landes- und Hochschulbibliothek, MS 733. For a full description of these two manuscripts, see UNDHAGEN (1978) and additional observations by Am (1992). 36 Cf. Am (1992:27). 37 UNDHAGEN (1978:118 with note 2). 3' 32 
 
 
 
 
 28 zo Farnese in Rome, geographically situated only a stone's throw from where St. Birgit- ta spent her last years, in the house still in existence as the Casa di Santa Brigida. The tradition of the text is complicated by many unknown factors, and it has not proven possible to draw up an unequivocal stemma codicum: instead we have found ev- idence of fixed relationships between some manuscripts, and floating affiliations between others. 2.2 The Archetype The modern editors of the various books of the Liber celestis generally appear to agree that the manuscript tradition can be traced back to one single common ancestor, the archetype; and that, at an early stage, the textual tradition was split up into two main branches, named and Tr.38 The former branch is represented by many surviving manuscripts originally produced in Italy, Germany or England.39 The extant manuscripts of the latter branch are, with a few important exceptions, of East European or Swedish provenance. The Liber ad reges offers a relatively slim basis for establishing the relationship of the manuscripts collated, as it contains fifty-eight chapters, of which no less than twenty-five are geminated ones, originally belonging to the Liber celestis and transposed with edito- rial changes into the Liber ad Reges. As Table 1 shows, these chapters have not received the same treatment in all manuscripts, as some give the texts in both Liber celestis and Liber ad reges, others more or less consistently give Rubric and Incipit in Liber celestis and then break off the text with a reference to the corresponding chapter in Liber ad reges, while Gh, the Editio princeps, follows the opposite rule: in Liber ad reges, the reader is referred back to Liber celestis. This means that the Apparatus criticus of the present edition reports two variants of the text given by Gh: first of all that given in Liber ad reges, until that text is broken off, and from then on the text given in Liber celestis. A further complication present in the geminated chapters is that, when assessing the value of the textual evidence culled from the text of these chapters,4° we must constant- ly keep in mind the possibility of interference from the same chapters of the earlier books. A number of textual errors, unique to the tradition of the Liber ad reges, can be found in all the collated manuscripts, demonstrating that the text of this book does, indeed, stem from one common ancestor or archetype. 22:3 omnibus, qui consilio, facto vel fauore dederunt operam in morte patris vestri, regis Petri: vestri] tui rodd. — this is a geminated chapter; save for the error under dis- See Am (1992:32ff.), BERGH (1967:22ff.; 1971:34ff.; 1991:13ff), A.-M. JÖNSSON (1998:42ff.), and UNDHA- (1978:50ff.). For a general introduction to the history of the manuscripts and the textual tradition, see the editions men- tioned in note 1, particularly UNDHAGEN (1978:1ff.); the Prague manuscript u was first described in print by Aili (1992:28-30). The history of the three oldest, Italian manuscripts, viz. Tb, Y and P, will be traced with a new theory by AHA (AILI-SVANBERG, Imagines, forthcoming). 40 The Rubrics of the Liber ad reges are unique to this book, written by Alfonso for the new purpose the texts were to serve. 38 GEN

As the whole revelation is in the form of a letter, ad- dressed to two narred persons of royal rank, there can be no doubt that the deferential form vestri was the reading of the original; hence tui must be an error that was produced in the archetype of Book VIII and retained in the manuscripts giving this book. 34:38 aliquid exclusi, deest Gh2 — the indefinite pronoun aliquid is quite superfluous in the context and is, moreover, completely absent from the corresponding text of Rev. The word extra (§15) made for an obvious scribal correction, although u found a way of its own. 48:96 restinxisse scripsi BU secutus restrinxisse codd. Gh restinxisse in restrinxisse (sic!) mut. q — seeing that the subject and object of this verb are rex and venenum, I con- sider restinguo ("quench, assuage") a much more reasonable verb than restringo ("bind fast, restrict"), a correction where I was inspired by the Old Swedish translators. Hence, the Latin text of the manuscripts appears to be an error of the archetype. 48:164 et statim laqueus impositus fuit regi nolenti facere graciam homini: regi nolenti scripsi u secutus rege nolente codd. The scribe of u most probably reacted against this archetype error and found a plausible correction. 2.3 The hyparchetypes, 3 and Tr, and their subdivisions The modern editors of Books I to VII of the Revelations are in general agreement, albeit with some reservation,4I that the manuscripts regularly identify themselves, by shared er- rors, as belonging to four different sub-groups or branches, pi, pr 'y, and o..42 The 131 and P2 branches are subordinate to the [3 hyparchetype, while the two other branches in the same manner derive from the i-r hyparchetype. The manuscripts collated for Liber ad Reges on the whole conform with the above observations, while nonetheless offering a number of confusing items, as can be seen below. 2.3.1 The hyparchetype: and the [31 and [3.2 branches B P Y Ab r, Only one error has been found to group these five manuscripts together: 34:81 baptismatis om. B P Y Ab r — fonte baptismatis has a certain flavour of Alfon- 41 Cf. ALL, (1992:32ff.). 42 The cr group (ALL, 1992, BERGH 1967 and 1991, A.-M. JÖNSSON 1998) is dubbed 8 by UNDHAGEN (1978).

Three further instances merit discussion, although they offer no really conciusive evi- dence: 4:14 assentantibus] assencientibus B Y Ab r — the verb assentor is here clearly used in the classical sense of `saying "yes"' or `flattering' ; this is confirmed by the same verbal stem being repeated as a noun in §15 adulatores et assentatores. Assentior would give a much weaker meaning of merely "agreeing". Nonetheless, this instance is more of a vari- ant reading than a true conjunctive error. P, probably by cross-reference with another ex- emplar, gives the better reading. 30:1 abducta F K N Ab ct u Gh obducta B P Y r — obducta constitutes a clear textual error. The four manuscripts retaining it must therefore be considered as having received it from a common exemplar.  32 that one group is subordinate to this hyparchetype, namely K N u, sharing significant er- rors in many chapters of the Liber ad reges. There is no really clear evidence as to rela- tionship of F and q, either to one another or to the other manuscripts of the group. In one short section of chapter 56, there is a surprising correspondence in errors between F and N. 2.3.3 Complexities in the transmission of the text In the evaluation of the textual evidence, given the premiss that we are trying to establish the state of the text when Alfonso had finished his revision, it is necessary to distinguish between two groups of readings: those that constitute evidence concerning the avenues by which the text was handed down (the textual tradition), and those that provide insights into the ways along which Alfonso's revisions reached various manuscripts (the history of the text). This is particularly important in the case of the earliest Italian codices, for it is quite possible that manuscripts, although descendants from the same exemplar, may be divided in time so that some of them give the text of an uncorrected or unrevised exem- plar, while the rest give the text in a corrected state. The apparatus criticus of the present edition abounds with notices on the activities of the second hand of Y (denoted Y2), that has recorded variant readings culled from a separate exemplar. The Palermo manuscript, P, is known for its vacillating loyalties: it is identified as a [3 manuscript in Book I to VI (UNDHAGEN 1978, A.-M. JÖNSSON 1998, Am 1992, BERGH 1971 and 1991), although it is noted that readings from the IT branch keep appearing (BERGH 1971:54, 1991:16). In Book VII, it is a 'rr manuscript, belonging to the o- hyparchetype, along with F K q (BERGH 1967:59f.). In the present edition, its position is even more complicated, as it shows no consistent loyalties. There are, first of all, some instances where P, Y, and r share the same reading, against the testimony of the other manuscripts. However, the majority of these instances belong to geminated chapters, and when we compare the text of Rev. VIII with that of the earli- er book, we find that the text of Y (with P or r) is often, but not always, in agreement with that of the earlier book, that is, with a version less affected by Alfonso's correcting hand. The following examples will suffice: 13:11 ex caritate om. P in marg. suppl. Y2 suppl. r — these two words constitute an Al- fonsine addition and do not form part of the text handed down in Book IV 4. When Y and r were corrected against a second exemplar, the Alfonsine addition was entered. In P the omission escaped notice. 24:9 superbus aut B F P q u superbus et K N suspectus et Y r Rev. III 3 1 :9 suspectus aut Gh — the lemma represents an Alfonsine emendation — suspectus is, indeed, a curious word in the context but the text of the original version (III 31) offers no variants and is faithfully reported by Y r, but not by P. 27:8 ambo pleni sunt B K N q u ambo plene sunt Y r ambo sunt plene P — the text of- fered by the latter three manuscripts comes close to constituting a binding error. In the geminated chapter, Rev. IV 104, the situation is complicated by variations in the word or- der: ambo sunt pleni V ambe sunt pleni PY ambo pleni sunt BOAb pleni sunt ambo F 
 
 
 
 
 33 ambe plene sunt Gh (the text of this chapter of Book IV is missing in Kux K2 q): clear- ly, the task of finding the correct referent to plenus and of conjugating ambo correctly was beyond the competence of many of the scribes, possibly also of Alfonso. If both words were originally in the masculine, and Alfonso changed pleni into plene in order to make it refer to the bestiae (but failed to follow up his correction by changing ambo in- to ambe), the variants given by the manuscripts can be explained as attempts at correct- ing either the error of referent or that of gender, as the kings are much more logical ref- erents in the context. 34:69 hec autem confidencia Y r Rev. IV 74 hec autem iusticia B Ku N P q 34:70 sacrificium habent Y r Rev. IV 74 om. B Ku N P q. In these last two instances Al- fonso appears to have introduced errors into his own revision. The errors adduced on the whole make it reasonable to put P among the p manuscripts, but it is difficult to find evidence placing it in either of the two branches, pi and P2. There are, furthermore, two instances that link P with K N u instead, thus contradict- ing the evidence identifying P as a p manuscript: 23:35 in suppliciis om. K N u in marg. suppl. P 34:40 iudicio sit misericordia et iusticia B F Y q r Gh iusticia sit misericordia et seueri- tas K N P u As we are forced to conclude that the scribe of P used more than one exemplar for his text, this manuscript cannot be regarded as a true representative of either branch of the tradition, and its readings can only be evaluated in the light of their relationship to the rest of the evidence. Complexities are also evident when we study readings shared by P Y only: 23:7 stante lege currente in marg. suppl. Y2 om. P et Rev. VII 19:7 34:75 sine dei offensa in marg. suppl. Y2 om. P et Rev. IV 34:75 34:83 apercionis glorie in marg. suppl. Y2 om. P et Rev. IV 34:83 41:3 in archa noe post corr. Y2 in diluuio P — in Rev. IV 3, the manuscripts present a con- fused picture: in diluuio in archa (in archa del.) Y in diluuio PV BOAb q in archa noe K2 Gh, and I considered the original reading of Y the one most likely to have been the orig- inal, the absence of in archa being an error by omission in the majority of the manuscripts. In the present text we may note that in archa noe was clearly Alfonso's correction. The above errors, shared by P and the uncorrected Y against the other manuscripts, are omissions of phrases (probably Alfonsine additions to the Liber ad reges as they are not present in any older version in Rev. IV and VII) or obvious textual errors. They are all of a quality that would, in the general run, prove that these two manuscripts are related vis- a-vis the others. The answer is probably that they are inherently conservative and some- times retain readings belonging to the pre-Alfonsine text. Thus, P seems in these in- stances unaware of Alfonso's corrections (unless its scribe deliberately omitted them), while Y has been corrected by Y2 into an Alfonsine state. The examples cited above tend towards the conclusion that the readings of P Y r must be treated with a certain circumspection whenever they oppose the readings of the rest of the manuscripts. On the one hand, in the case of variant readings, we should expect the manuscript majority B F K N P q u to offer a later — and therefore better — Alfonsine text; but on the other hand, whenever the majority offers an indubitable error, we should seek support for a correct reading in P Y r, particularly whenever these three are in agreement with the tradition of the earlier books. 
 
 
 
 
 34 The evidence on the whole encourages us to conclude that two branches, f31 and j32, do exist in the manuscript tradition of the Liber ad reges. However, we are left in consider- able doubt as to whether these two branches can really be united under a p hyparchetype. 2.4 Conciusion The building of a stemma codicum for the Liber ad reges, on the strength of the textual evidence, has not proven fruitful. We have been able to verify that all the manuscripts col- lated have ultimately descended from a common exemplar, the archetype. From the op- posite point of view we have been able to demonstrate the existence of three sub-group- ings below the level of primary hyparchetypes, namely 131, 132, and y. What is less clear is, whether 131 and P2 can be united under the shared hyparchetype that has been attested in the earlier books and dubbed P; furthermore, although we have some evidence that F K N P q u stem from the Tr hyparchetype, we are left in the dark concerning the exact relationship between the y manuscripts and F P and q. We have, finally, found evidence that points at at a close relationship between P and Y; on the other hand, we have found evidence that the writer of P used more than one exemplar for his text and that he used them without any clear principle. The section dubbed Codices adhibiti will therefore not offer a stemma codicum but on- ly a summary of the above results. A more fruitful approach is probably to discuss the history of the manuscripts themselves, taking the different readings into account. A new theory on the interrelationship of the manuscripts will therefore be offered by Mu (forth- coming). 
 
 
 
 
 3. Textual criticism This section discusses a number of typical problems encountered during the editorial work, including passages where the text has been emended against the manuscript evi- dence. The process of editing a Latin text from the manuscripts involves continuous crit- icism and internal discussion of the text and the question of its original wording, and the present selection makes no claim to be a complete review of all problematical passages. The apparatus criticus will offer the sources for the reader's own discussion of Birgitta's text. 6:1 diligit eam, si eam P Y r ostendit se diligere eam, quando eam B F q Gh ostendit se diligere eum quando eum K N u — the readings of the majority of the manuscripts appear as ill-considered Alfonsine changes, as they fail to take into account the long subsequent series of conditional si-clauses which ought also to have been changed into quando- clauses. 8:19 ventris K N u q Gh2 venter F B P Y r (-er in ras. F) — did Alfonso plan, but fail to carry through, a revision? Huius ventris, id est fidei tue, parietes fuerunt quasi aurum ful- gentissimum is a rather unfelicitious phrase, considerably inferior to the sense I believe to have been the intended one: Iste venter, id est fidei tue parietes, fuit quasi aurum ful- gentissimum. In the absence of more conclusive evidence we will have to let the wording stand in the original form it had in Rev. V rev.4:19,43 as huius itaque venter is clearly im- possible. 12:1 est scripsi K (ante corr.) N u secutus esset B F P Y q Gh — esset is the text of the archetype, and almost certainly an error, as the context offers no clear reason for the im- perfect subjunctive. 13:10 stabilis esto in homine accepto B F Y q r u stabilis esto in honore accepto K N P Gh2 — the variant reading is honore; in the present book, homine was clearly the reading of the archetype. In my edition of Book IV (ch. 4:10) I printed homine with a certain trep- idation, as it was only supported by V Y against the majority of the manuscripts. My choice was made at the very last moment before printing,44 and no time or space was available for a discussion, a defect which must be remedied here. Cf. BERGH 1971:76. The very strong manuscript evidence in favour of honore had made this seem the natural and uncontrover- sial, albeit not very elegant, reading; when I was putting the finishing touches to the edition of Rev. IV I had started collating the text of Book VIII, and then realised that the manuscript evidence for homine was actually quite strong, a fact which made me revise my interpretation of the text according to the lines given here. 43 44 
 
 
 
 
 36 First of all, there can be no doubt that we are dealing with variant readings, neither of which can be designated a textual error, although homine is in many ways the lectio dif- ficilior. Good arguments can be advanced for both readings: the word honor occurs twice in the preceding paragraphs, a fact that could explain the exchange of homine for honore — but then, we find the word homines only a couple of lines further down, and one might argue that the scribe took in this word prematurely. Secondly, paragraphs eight and nine indubitably discuss the adverse effects on the Queen's honour, if she follows her inclination and departs on a pilgrimage, and we might expect the question of honour to be carried on in paragraph ten — on the other hand the argumentation is closed with the end of paragraph nine, and in paragraph ten the Devil switches to a new track of argument. The word homine offers a certain progression in his arguments,45 which lead from the Queen herself, via her outward status, to her whole house: 'Stand fast in relation to the man you have accepted, hold fast to your condition in a regal fashion, order your household in a laudable way.' If, however, we choose hono- re we shall credit Birgitta's Devil with no capacity for rhetorical elegance46 but only for partial, and therefore ineffectual, repetition: 'Stand fast in the honour you have received' etc., the words lonour' and 'rank' being here more or less synonymous. It might furthermore be said against homine that this word presupposes the existence of a husband, while no such person is mentioned in the preceding text. This, in my view, is wholly a result of Alfonso's failure to carry through his adaptation of this text into its new context: in the older version given in Book IV, the lady is mentioned from the out- set as being a domina (that is, a married lady), not a queen;47 in the version of Book VIII, owing to the consistent use of regina for domina, her married state is not mentioned explicitly until in paragraph 37: this fact strengthens the arguments for homine, as no scribe would find reason, given the text of paragraphs seven to ten, wilfully to use this word in preference for honore. Finally, I would argue that the word homine, when retained, adds psychological depth to the Devil's method of offering temptations: we might be surprised to find him de- fending the sanctity of the married state, as he does when he urges this Queen to remain in her marriage rather than leaving the country on a pilgrimage. But since the same pil- grimage is represented, by the Revelation's Good Spirit, as the most efficacious means of saving her soul, the Devil's own arguments reveal him as not only a master of stratagem in the struggle for souls, but even as a strategist, capable of accepting a temporary set- back in exchange for a long-term gain. Classical illustrations to this movement within a rhetorical structure are not hard to find; cf., for instance Ci- cero, Cat. 11 Nihilne te nocturnum praesidium Palatii nihil horum ora vultusque moverunt? where Cicero leads Catiline on an imaginary tour, studded with anaphors, from the Palatine hill down into the City, out among the people, through the concourse of patriots around the Senate, into the fortified temple of Iuppiter Stator, even to up the worried expressions of the senators themselves. 46 In this Revelation, the Devil does present his case rather elegantly and with a certain predilection for tricolon and antithesis (as in paragraph 8: tu seruies minuetur honor tuus et augebitur despeccio). More instances can be found in his subsequent utterances. 47 The retention of regaliter is another example of Alfonso's failure in carrying through his changes. The ad- verb is correct in the context of the older version, but in the Liber ad reges it ought to have been replaced by regalem, 'hold fast to your royal rank'. 
 
 
 
 
 37 15:16 qui occidet et iudicabit codd. Gh2 — in Rev. IV 17:16, I followed V Y against all the other manuscripts and printed viuificabit, viewing this place as Birgitta's reminis- cence of 1 Sm. 2,6 Dominus mortificat et vivificat and 4 Rg. 5,7 Numquid Deus ego sum, ut occidere possim et vivificare. I thus regarded the whole passage as a description of the might of the Creator. In the present book, however, complete consensus reigns among the manuscripts for iudicabit." It is not possible to disregard this testimony, and since it is perfectly possible to consider that the passage was altered by Alfonso into dealing with the wrath, rather than the power, of the Creator, and iudicabit is consonant with this read- ing, it is best left standing. 15:19 mundum scripsi mundo B K N P Y q r u om. Gh2 — the ablative after ingredi in is not attested anywhere else in this book of the Revelations, nor have I observed it in any other Revelation; hence, its occurrence here appears to be a simple error in the archetype. It is rather remarkable, however, that it has not been corrected by any scribe. 15:19 subiturus scripsi facturus B K N P Y q r u Gh2 — cf. my comments on Rev. IV 17:19. 16:4 ille vellera habet ouina: vellera F K q Gh verba B N P Y r u— verba appears as the reading of the archetype, in all probability an early scribal error, as verba ouina would mean that Birgitta considered the counsellor in question a wolf who bleated like a sheep. Hence, I have accepted vellera, regarding it as a compelling scribal correction giving the text the meaning that the counsellor is a wolf in sheep's clothing, a comment that is per- fectly apposite in its context. 16:7-8. This Addicio is only given by F K2 Gh, that is, by two Vadstena manuscripts and by the printed edition dependent upon these. F offers the addicio written en suite with the main text, while K does not mention it suo loco but only at the very end of the codex (fol. 260r) and then written by K2. Gh adopts the additional text fully into the revelation it- self, without informing the reader of its real status. 17:6-7. This Addicio is in every respect comparable with the preceding one. The best treatment of these Addiciones is a delicate matter, as we must take two con- flicting observations into account: first of all, that it cannot have been Alfonso who in- cluded them into the Liber ad reges — this is obvious from their absence in all but the Vad- stena manuscripts — instead, they were almost certainly added by the editors at Vadstena Abbey, in the belief that these two snippets of Revelations would fit into these two loca- tions; secondly, they were almost certainly written by Birgitta herself and therefore prob- ably belong to those texts which strayed outside the Revelations corpus proper. In other words, we do not doubt their authentic wording, only the authenticity of their placing. It is, indeed, difficult to see why the editors found it appropriate to insert the addition to 16 where they did, as its only clear connexion with the main text is the mentioning of It is quite possible that viuificabit was a scribe's reminiscence of the two passages of the Vulgate, that is, a kind of scribal `Verschlimmbesserung'. 48 
 
 
 
 
 38 a `wolf , apparently referring to the king's counsellor mentioned in paragraph 2. The main subject-matter of the addition is an attack on a domina (not mentioned in the main text) of royal rank who has made a false marriage: this topic, however, is discussed at greater length in 11. To complicate matters further, the final words of the addition seem to refer to 41, particularly paragraphs 30 to 32. The addition to 17, on the other hand, is much more appositely placed and clearly refers to the same subject matter as the main text, although it indicates that the advice given had not been followed, and that the for- eign counsellor had been allowed to stay. As it seems fairly sure that these two texts are by Birgitta herself, we cannot exclude or athetize them from the place they have held ever since the writing of the Codices Falkenberg et Kalmarnensis. On the other hand, their lack of authenticity in reference to Alfonso's plans must be clearly indicated in the printing. Hence, they are given here in a smaller typeface. 18:20 superbia scripsi BU secutus superbie cornibus rodd. Gh — BU has reacted against the unfortunate parable of dogs sporting antlers and has corrected the text, probably against its Latin source, by writing `omättelike hunda hulke som vane äru at hata faarin spiärna them mz thera höghfärdh'. The Latin archetype has superbie cornibus, which might be an Alfonsine error, committed through a reminiscence of Greg. Magn. B F K N q u Gh — the absence of these words in the majority of the manuscripts is probably due on- ly to a saut du n-ttrze au måne (per corpus ... per Ihesum). 19:10 Si vero obedire noluerit (voluerit K N P) sicut Filius meus dixerat sibi, dimittat (+ dolere K N + dolorem u), quia nos bene inueniemus vias per quas — The text is not crystal clear as the wording stands in the majority of the manuscripts and in Gh. My in- terpretation is: `But even if he is unwilling to obey as my Son told him to do, he ought to yield, for we will certainly find ways etc.' If we accept voluerit, we would have to add he ought to relieve himself of his dolere or an equivalent Cif he is willing to obey pain'): this corresponds to the text of BU, also adopted by LUNDEN. 24:12 reuiuixit B P Y q reuixit F K N u Gh2 reuirexit ut vid. r — the perfect tense form reuiuixit (for reuixit) was probably made by the original translator and corrected by var- ious scribes (its presence in q suggests that Alfonso did not correct it himself). Its later reappearance in Sweden is attested by GMLS, and I have thought it reasonable to retain it here as an instance of Swedish Latinity. 27:13 produnt] predunt (i.q. preduntur) exspectes — The textual tradition is unanimous, both in Rev. IV and VIII. On the other hand, seeing that the construction prodere aliquid de aliquo has no precedents, whereas praedor (or praedo) aliquid de aliquo "rob some- thing from someone" appears possible (cf. L&S), we might regard produnt as a simple misreading for praedunt. 
 
 
 
 
 39 27:17 qui cotidie rogant P u que cotidie rogant BY q r u que cotidie rogat K N Gh2 — a double fault of Alfonso, who not only introduced the words et popull into the text but al- so (by a constructio ad sensum) put the verb into the plural form, but falled to follow up the congruence between the relative pronoun and its new antecedent. 34:40 in omni iusticia sit misericordia et seueritas K N P u in omni iudicio sit miseri- cordia et iusticia BY q r in iudicio sit misericordia et iusticia Rev. IV et Gh2 — the word- ing adopted suggests that the hand of Alfonso has been active here: hence we are justi- fied in adopting the reading most different from the tradition of Rev. IV. 34:47 inferiora ante dignior habent B r Gh2 in marg. suppl. Y2 — the fact that this word is absent from the majority of the manuscripts probably means that it was deleted by Al- fonso. An analogous instance is offered by 34:70. 34:69 hec autem confidencia Y r (confidencia in ras. per Y2 scriptum vid.) Gh2 hec autem iusticia B K N P q u — the evidence offered by the manuscript majority suggests that Alfonso introduced an error into his text here. 34:88 me ante expendisse habet Gh2 om. codd. — just as in Rev. IV, I have considered the correction offered by Gh2 sound and necessary. 37:14 intus: in tuis B P Y q intus K N r Gh2 meus u — the distribution of the readings among the manuscripts leaves little doubt that in tuis was the the reading of the archetype of the Liber ad reges; within its context it is almost meaningless, a fact which has prompt- ed the scribes into two different corrections, intus and meus, the former of which has the merit of corresponding with and providing a contrast against extra in the paragraph that follows. Alternative corrections could be formed on the hypothesis that a word has been omitted in the archetype; in this case, moribus could be supplied after in tuis (partly sup- ported by Rev. VI 53:4 ut et desiderabilis sit moribus, where the context unfortunately is far different). As the reading offered by K N r is also given by Y in Rev. II 11:14, it ap- pears as the most authoritative correction of the archetype error. 42:8 Quod serui audientes nunciauerunt hoc (hoc om. K) domino suo codd. Gh — hoc is clearly pleonastic, but it is retained in the text, since a parallel instance, containing a re- dundant hoc, is offered by VII 12:13 quod siue hoc fecerunt propter supplicacionem siue propter negligenciam. BERGH (1967:101) discusses this paragraph from another point of view without mentioning this redundancy. 43:4 Si enim rex ... nec incipiat bonum — this passage offers two main versions, a short one given by F K N P q u and Gh and a much longer one given B Y r. It is not possible to see how the text could have been so radically abbreviated by three separate scribal mis- takes, as none of them creates an unintelligible text. The distribution of the readings in the manuscripts therefore suggests that the longer version is Birgitta's original one and that Alfonso here cut down the text radically, apparently in order to remove any mention of war, and thereby turning Birgitta's plans for a crusade into instructions for a peaceful parley. Moreover, B Y r lack the word stabiliter, which has the characteristic air of offi- 
 
 
 
 
 40 LIBRI SECUNDI CAP. 1 cious Alfonsine paedagogy. The fact that P, normally so conservative, here follows the Alfonsine correction is a hint that this scribe actually chose between alternative readings and did not merely follow the readings of his exemplar. As this passage offers a most in- tricate problem, where I cannot claim to be able to offer a definite solution, I have here made the apparatus criticus more explicit than usual, to provide the reader with more in- formation. 47:6 Ergo passionem Filii mei comparo tonitruo, cuius aduentus prius auditur et longe post, quam sonus venit — I agree with LUNDtl\l'S interpretation (1956 III 299) "Jag jäm- för min Sons lidande med ett tordön, vars annalkande höres innan knallen kommer och långt efteråt"), in English (additions within brackets are my own), "I compare the passion of my Son with a thunderstorm, the advent of which is heard (as a rumble) before the thunder (of lightning), and long after". The corrections offered by various mss. are, there- fore, hardly necessary. 48:21 quod plures participarentur bonitatis (bonitate B Y r u) — participo(r) is normally transitive, but the genitive attested by the manuscript majority is probably due to influ- ence from the construction particeps alicuius rei. The ablative in its tum could be a more learned reminiscence from the Vulgate (2 Macc. 5,10 patrio sepulcro participans). 52: 4 ex toto corde B P Y r Gh toto corde F K N q u — the Revelations offer plenty of examples of toto corde (in the pure ablative), while the adverbial construction ex toto is also very frequent; ex toto corde, seen in this light, appears as a kind of contaminated construction, also attested in Rev. II 23:2; IV 6:6, VII 20:30 and 27:4. 54:4 despeccionis F K N u Gh despecto B P Y q r— the distribution of readings in the manuscripts indicates that despecto was the reading of the archetype, possibly the result of a failure to understand an abbreviation. There can be no doubt that despeccionis is the correct reading, as this word occurs several times as one of a pair of near-synonyms, as for instance Rev. II 23:13 pro obprobrio ... et despeccione, IV 74:61 despeccio et aduer- sitas mundi, VI 18:4 in paupertate sua et despeccione. 55:7 pontes B F K P Y Gh potentes N q r u— the passage as a whole is an exhortation to God to enter into his reign while putting obstacles in the way of various classes of peo- ple; hence, a final injunction to throw down the bridges before the commonalty is more consonant with this exhortation than the revolutionary message offered by potentes. 56R et faciem B K N P Y q u et in eo faciem humanam Y2 r Gh — could the reading of Y2 r be a very late revision by Alfonso? If so, it could only have been entered into the two manuscripts with known connexions to his residence during his final years (cfr. 56:74 in agno apparebat facies una hominis). This could explain its presence in Gh. At all ac- counts, the reading of the majority is probably that of the archetype. 56:3 qui F K N u qua B Y q r Gh quam P — qua is most probably the reading of the archetype, but is unreasonable in the context, as it must refer to potencia et voluntate ("I am still the same in power and will, in which I was when ..."), not to the obvious an- 
 
 
 
 
 LIBRI SECUNDI CAP. 1 41 tecedent ego idem et ille ("I am still the same as I was when"). Hence, qui appears a good correction made early in the textual tradition. The construction, ego idem sum qui (in- stead of idem sum atque) is weil attested in the Birgittine corpus: cf. for example Rev I 2:3 and 41:2. 56:14 et laudantibus K laudantibus N u et laudancium B P Y r q laudancium F Gh — this is in reality two problems, namely that concerning the retention or omission of the con- nective et and that of the choice between laudancium and laudantibus. The connective makes the present participle a noun, which is not unheard of in the Revelations (cf. for instance 4:14 Innitatur eciam consiliis sapientum et expertorum et timencium Deum). In the absence of et, the participle retains its normal function as an attribute to seruitoribus, which is probably the reason for the omission of et in N u F Gh. In the Revelationes, plenus with the genitive is used very rarely (I 20:13; SA 5:20), whereas it is quite fre- quent when used with the ablative. Hence, even though et laudancium may have been the reading of the archetype, et laudantibus is much more in keeping with the normal lan- guage used. 58:13 horribili veneno B F N P q Gh2 veneno horribili K u horribili vento Y r — the read- ing of Y r probably indicates the inherent conservatism of these two manuscripts, as "wind", although far from polite, is more drastic, and probably more Birgittine, than the conventional "poison", which may then be an instance of Alfonso's bowdlerizing the text. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 4. Principles of Edition 4.1 Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges The present edition of the Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges marks the first complete ap- pearance in print of this Birgittine work. In the Ghotan edition and those later editions that depended on it, those twenty-five chapters that Alfonso had culled from the earlier books were truncated in the Liber ad reges, where only the Rubric and a short Incipit were given; the reader being referred back to the relevant parts of the earlier books. A representation of this process of referring the reader forwards or backwards is given in Table 1. In the present edition we actually revert to the principle observed in the earliest, Italian manuscripts, and give the full text of each chapter. The capital letters within brackets in the present text represent the partitioning offered in the Ghotan edition, where these letters are printed within the text itself. The orthography of here follows the same principles as in my edition of book IV, in the sense that I mainly follow the orthography of the Codex Kalmarnensis, except that vocalic u is printed as u not v, even in an initial position. True consistency is very diffi- cult to achieve, not least because orthographic variants abound in all manuscripts. There is one, consistent divergence from the orthography of K, and that is the spelling of the name of the author herself: Birgitta is the normal Swedish spelling, also given by manuscripts of Swedish origins, while the normal spelling in Italian manuscripts is Brigi- da. Seeing that this book was edited by Alfonso, a Spaniard, I considered it reasonable to maintain the form of this name that was normal to him. However, the name only occurs twice, namely in the Incipit and the Explicit of the work. The purpose of the apparatus criticus is to enable the reader to criticize the readings adopted in my edition, and to follow the main traits of the history of the text. Hence the apparatus does not report every variant spelling or reading given by the manuscripts con- sulted but only those necessary for the purpose stated. In the initial stages of the work, the apparatus did, indeed report all variants of orthography and word order present in ev- ery manuscript, but during the editorial process readings that were deemed superfluous were removed individually. Hence, it is not possible or profitable to formulate a single, consistent principle for the readings offered by the finished product, except that my ob- ject, as editor, has been to offer the reader a maximum of information in a minimum of space. In the apparatus criticus itself the manuscripts collated are reported in alphabetical or- der by their sigla. The discussion concerning their mutual relationship (above, section 1.2), did not lead to an unequivocal conclusion or an arrangement of the manuscript into sub - groups, and I therefore consider this alphabetical arrangement the best way to serve the reader's criticism of the text printed. 
 
 
 
 
 44 The apparatus fontium is — with a few exceptions — an apparatus on Biblical sources and gives not only my own findings — which are relatively few in number — but also re- peats those of earlier Birgittine scholars, that is KLOCKARS and the modern editors. The greatest part of the credit for finding these sources therefore goes to them. The text offered for reading is the version which in my opinion can be considered to constitute Alfonso's final choice as self-appointed editor of the Liber ad reges. In the oth- er Birgittine works, the modern editors' object was to reconstruct the most authentic text, that is, the text closest to Birgitta's original — bearing in mind that this original was nor- mally expressed in Swedish, the editors aimed to reproduce the text in the shape it re- ceived when it had left the two translators' writing desk and had been checked and cor- rected by Alfonso. The Liber ad reges, however, is the result of Alfonso's conscious re- vision of fifty-eight Revelations into a Speculum regale. Hence, we are now less con- cerned with the question of Birgitta's original wording and more with that of Alfonso's intentions when he revised the texts. In most instances, this means that I have followed readings unanimously offered by the majority, in particular when this is represented by B F K N P q u Gh and these differ from those offered by P Y r. Naturally, whenever the reading of the majority has appeared to me doubtful, I have not hesitated to avail myself of the readings of the latter group or of the Old Swedish translators or, in the final in- stance, of my own opinion. 4.2. Epistola solitarii ad reges For the sake of completeness, a reprint of Alfonso's own preface, entitled Epistola soli- tarii ad reges (ES), is printed before the edition of the Liber celestis ad reges proper. The ES is reprinted by permission of the editor, Dr. Arne JÖNSSON, Lund University, who pub- lished this text in his doctoral dissertation of 1989.49 The text of the ES is reprinted with the normalized orthography used by Dr. JöNssoN,5° and the reader will note that this dif- fers in minor respects from that used in the Liber ad reges. In conformity with the ex- pressed wish of the Birgitta Committee of the Royal Academy of Letters, History and An- tiquities of Stockholm, the text of ES is also furnished with an apparatus criticus, citing the manuscripts used by Dr. JÖNSSON for his edition, the difference being that JÖNSSON uses x and y while I use u alone for the ? group; JÖNSSON uses B 0 Ab for the 132 group (I use only B, as 0 lacks this text and I deemed Ab of minor interest). F lacks ES ch. 1-7 and was left uncollated by JÖNSSON in ES ch. 8; in the present reprint, I have for the sake of completeness added my own collation notes on F and N, as I collated these two manuscripts in Book VIII. The addition of their readings does not change the wording of the text as edited by Dr. JÖNSSON. As Dr. JöNssoN's original apparatus criticus — unlike his text of the ES — was not avail- able in electronic form, I had to transcribe it manually from the original edition, taking the opportunity of giving it the same form as I use in my edition of Book VIII: hence, the collation itself is by Dr. JÖNSSON, while the information is presented in the same fashion ay Reviewed (in the Swedish language) by Am (1991). 50 JÖNSSON (1989:113): "I have carried out certain normalizations of spelling in accordance with the usage gen- erally observed in the manuscripts." 
 
 
 
 
 45 as in the edition of the Liber ad reges, the main differences being that instead of using, for instance, the siglum as an abbreviation for B 0 Y Ab r, I print all the manuscript sigla in full in alphabetical order. In conformity with the principle I have employed in my own apparatur, I leave out readings (errors or variants) given by only one manuscript, unless this reading appears particularly interesting. In the one instance where I have printed a reading different from that given in Dr. JÖNS- SON'S edition of the text of ES (ch. 1:1), I have done so with his written permission.

proch dolor, euenerunt ex impericia istius tam secrete et tam inusitate materie. 10 Et quia ista Bea- ta Bomina, cui presens liber diuinitus reuelatus fult in visione, dicit in prin- cipio eius, quod vidit in visione palacium grande, incomprehensibile mag- nitudine, simile celo sereno, etc., 11 idcirco ego nunc decreui predictum tractatum seu ventilabrum hic per modum epistole compilare, quo visiones bone et diuine a dyabolicis et malignis, velut granum a palea, excuciantur et subtiliter separentur, 12 vt sic granum purum et mundum in orreo spi- ritualium et catholicorum hominum recondatur et veneretur, palea vero dyabolica illusionum a vento flante Diuine Scripture longius in sterquili- nium proiciatur et pedibus conculcetur. 13 Incipiens igitur in Christi nomine, semper subiciens omnia, que dixero, correccioni sancte matris Ecclesie et consilio saniori, 14 dico quod, qui species visionum seu reuelacionum iuste et discrete examinare et discutere ac discernere et iuste iudicare vult, 15 oportet, quod habeat duo supradicta, scilicet theoricam sciencie Sacre Scripture in materia vi- sionum, quam sancti patres et doctores subtiliter et lucide descripserunt, et practicam experiencie vite spiritualis et sentimentorum consolacionum et visionum mentalium spiritualiter aut intellectualiter sibi diuinitus infuso- rum. 16 Et quia communiter hodie in paucis CAP. 1-2 51 siue ministrate sint — 29 quia videmus, quod Pharao non credidit Moysi, qui homo simplex erat et Deum viderat, et ideo eius visioni et dictis, vel pocius Dei, non credens in duricia incredulitatis pertinaciter perstitit, propter quod in Mari Rubro cum populo Egypti miserabiliter precipitauit, 30 et multos alios eciam vidi temporibus meis, qui tales spirituales per- sonas simplices et ydiotas et visiones earum spirituales vanas et quasi con- fictas aut fantasticas reputantes inobedientes Deo effecti sunt, 31 propter quod super se et subditos suos sentencias Dei crudelissimas, sibi per illas personas et earum visiones ante prenunciatas, miserabiliter experti sunt (32 exemplum vide in destruccione regni Cypri, quia non credidit princeps visionibus beate Brigide, cui presens liber extitit reuelatus, vt habetur in vltimo Libro Celesti, XIX capitulo; 33 alios eciam vidi et audiui, qui cre- debant illusionibus et ex hoc periculose corruerunt, 34 exemplum huius vide in VI Libro Celesti, LXVIII capitulo et in libro Collacionum Patrum Iohannis Cassiani, collacione II, capitulo "Et vt hane eandem" cum mul- tis similibus) — 35 ideo consulcius est visiones et personas tales cum magna maturitate discutere et eas tunc bene et cum deliberacione et matu- ritate discussas et discrete iudicatas aut approbare aut eciam reprobare. 36 Ait enim apostolus: Probate spiritus, vtrum a Deo sint. Et si tunc in ex- amine inuentum est, quod a Deo sint, debemus humiliter illas credere et eis totaliter obedire. 37 Si vero a dyabolo sint, vt illusiones pessimas illas debemus despicere et eis nullatenus fidem dare. Ponitur hic in theorica modus examinis faciendi de qualitate persone vi- dentis visiones et de modo habendi eas ac de qualitate materie visionum et reuelacionum.  EP. SOL. CAP. 4-5 63 mente sua in tantum, quod quasi nullatenus nisi cum suspiriis aut lacri- mis hoc michi referre poterat. Hic examinatur per sacram scripturam, sub qua specie visionum conti- neantur visiones istius libri et alie reuelate beate Brigide. Capitulum V. 1 Ex quo igitur iam certificaui dubitantes de qualitate et virtutibus persone prefate, domine Brigide beatissime, et de modis diuersis, quibus ipsa do- mina visiones et reuelaciones presentis libri et aliorum librorum videbat et audiebat atque senciebat, vt supra patet, 2 nunc autem restaret videre de qualitate materie ipsarum visionum et reuelacionum, 3 sed quia satis de il- la tetigi supra et tangetur infra in § "Hoc eciam" et in § "Ergo, o vos im- peratores" et eciam ex preiacente huius libri materia et aliorum librorum suorum patet euidenter, ideo non figo plus pedem in hoc. 4 Sed iam michi restat nunc examinare et declarando probare secundum Scripturam et dic- ta sanctorum doctorum, sub qua specie visionum communiter predicte sue visiones et reuelaciones contineantur. 5 Sciendum est ergo de primo, quod Augustinus in libro XII Super Ge- nesim ad Litteram et Ieronimus in prologo Apocalipsis tria principalia ge- nera visionum describunt, scilicet corporale, spirituale et intellectuale. 6 Vi- sio enim corporalis est, quando videlicet corporalibus oculis aliquid vide- mus. 7 Spiritualis seu ymaginaria visio dicitur, cum videlicet dormientes vel vigilantes in spiritu ymagines rerum cernimus, quibus aliquid aliud significatur, sicut vidit Pharao spicas et Moyses rubum ardere, ille dormiens et iste vigilans. 8 Intellectualis autem visio est, quando videlicet Spiritu Sancto reuelante intellectu mentis veritatem misteriorum, sicut est, capimus, quo modo vidit lohannes illa, que in libro Apocalipsis referun- tur. 9 Non enim figuras tantum in spiritu vidit, sed et eorum significata mente intellexit.  64 EP. SOL. CAP. 5 10 Primum namque genus visionum, scilicet corporale, non multum tangit materiam nostram, licet tamen aliquibus vicibus ipsa beata Brigida gloriosam matrem Dei et eius filium corporalibus oculis viderit, scilicet quando erat puella. 11 Vidit enim altare et matrem Dei supra sedentem, que vocauit eam et imposuit ei coronam, et iterum quando periclitabatur in partu et mater Dei intrauit ad eam et videntibus dominabus cunctis, que aderant, tetigit membra eius et statim liberata est, vt hoc habetur in legen- da vite eius, 12 et iterum quando vidit ignem descendere de celo super altare et in manu presbiteri celebrantis hostiam et in ea agnum et faciem hominis in agno et agnum in facie, et iterum vidit in manu presbiteri in hostia infantem viuum, qui signando astantes signo crucis dixit: 13 "Benedico vos credentes; non credentibus ero index." Vide hec in VI Li- bro Celesti, LXXXVI capitulo. 14 Cumque eciam ipsa iam ageret in ex- tremis, vidit iterum corporaliter Christum eam consolantem, vt supra di- citur. 15 Talis enim visio, credendum est, quod ab ipso Christo eidem sponse sue Spiritus Sancti ministerio in eius consolacionem fuerit ostensa et non a maligno spiritu illusorie ministrata. 16 Et hoc patet clare, quia ipse Dominus Iesus Christus predixerat ei diem sui exitus quinque diebus, antequam ipsa expiraret, precipiens ei sacramenta Ecclesie suscipere et de factis suis et monasterii sui aliqua per eum tunc reuelata disponendo or- dinare. 17 Quod preceptum in fine vite dyabolus ei non dedisset, si illa vi- sio dyabolica extitisset. 18 Probatur eciam, quod illa visio fuerit a Deo ex hoc, quia in illo extremo puncto vite ipsa communionem sanctam et ex- tremam vnccionem presentibus personis multis deuote recepit. 19 Quibus susceptis et ipsum Christum oculis corporalibus tunc eam consolantem vidit et in eius manibus spiritum humiliter commendando expirauit, vt hoc patet in vltimo Libro Celesti, capitulo vltimo et in fine legende vite eius. 20 In hoc tamen modo videndi visiones, scilicet corporaliter, non figo pe- dem pro nunc, quia ipsa domina paucis vicibus videbat visiones corporal- ibus oculis. 10 nostram om. x y istam K gloriosam] gloriosam virginem mariam B viderit] vidit x y 11 enim] etiam r aderat r et post partu om. CAP. 5 65 21 De secundo autem genere visionis, scilicet spiritualis seu ymagina- rie dormiencium non loquamur, quia ista sompnia suspectissima reputan- tur per Gregorium in quarto libro Dyalogorum, XLVIII capitulo. 22 Et ha- betur eciam idem in Libro IIII Celesti, XXXVIII capitulo, quamuis ali- quando sompnia sint vera et bona et a Deo, vt ibi dicitur. Et ibi in Libro Celesti vide modum, quem debes tenere, quando talia sompnia eueniunt. 23 Et tali modo dormiendo vidit eciam ista beata domina, cum adhuc puel- la esset, semel Christum quasi tunc temporis crucifixum, et ab illa hora semper affecta fuit cordialiter ad passionem Christi. Nec talis vislo somp- nialis tangit materiam nostram, 24 quia ipsa domina quasi omnes visiones videbat in oracione vigilando et non dormiendo, vt in multis capitulis pre- dicti Libri Celestis hoc patet euidenter. 25 Sed veniendo modo ad istam secundam speciem, quam dixi, scilicet visionis spiritualis seu ymaginarie vigilancium, que tangit materiam nos- tram, dicit sic beatus Augustinus in libro XII Super Genesim ad Litteram: 26 Quando autem penitus auertitur atque arripitur animi intencio a sen- sibus corporis, tunc magis dici extasis solet. Tunc enim omnino que- cumque sunt presencia corpora eciam patentibus oculis non videntur, 27 nec ulle voces prorsus audiuntur, quia totus animi contuitus aut in corpo- rum ymaginibus est per spiritualem aut in rebus incorporeis nulla cor- poris ymagine figuratis per intellectualem visionem. 28 Ex istis ergo ver- bis patet manifeste, quod quando dicta domina stabat in visione, sopita gaudio et sopore dulcedinis amoris diuini, alienata a sensibus corporis, vt supra ipsa narrat, quod tunc stabat in extasi alienata supra se, 29 nec talem dulcedinem et ebrietatem amoris diuini in alicuius mente dyabolus infun- dere potest, vt infra patebit, quia, quod ipse non habet, aliis infundere nequit. 30 Cum vero ipsa dicit supra et in libris, quod Deus animam suam tunc quasi a sompno excitabat ad videndum et audiendum atque sencien- dum celestia, diuina et spiritualia et quod tunc ipsa visiones videbat et vo- cem loquentis audiebat in spiritu, 31 tunc ostenditur clare per istud dictum 21 visionis] visionum K ista] et ista q per gregorium om. x y Gregorium] beatum gre- gorium B libro quarto K P q x y Gh 22 idem om.
